<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Indian Ocean</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the rock band, see <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_(band)" title="Indian Ocean (band)">Indian Ocean (band)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ocean bounded by Asia, Africa and Australia</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Body of water</div>

<p>The <b>Indian Ocean</b> is the third-largest of the world's five <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> divisions, covering 70,560,000 km<sup>2</sup> (27,240,000 sq mi) or ~19.8% of the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA-volume_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-volume-4">[4]</a></sup> It is bounded by <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> to the west and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a> to the east. To the south it is bounded by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> or <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, depending on the definition in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Along its core, the Indian Ocean has some large marginal or regional seas such as the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laccadive_Sea" title="Laccadive Sea">Laccadive Sea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somali_Sea" title="Somali Sea">Somali Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a>.
</p>

Arabia.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Indian Ocean has been known by its present name since at least 1515 when the Latin form <i>Oceanus Orientalis Indicus</i> ("Indian Eastern Ocean") is attested, named for India, which projects into it. It was earlier known as the <i>Eastern Ocean</i>, a term that was still in use during the mid-18th century (see map), as opposed to the <i>Western Ocean</i> (<a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>) before the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> was surmised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarperIndianOcean_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarperIndianOcean-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">Chinese explorers in the Indian Ocean</a> during the 15th century called it the Western Oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_geography" title="Ancient Greek geography">Ancient Greek geography</a>, the Indian Ocean region known to the Greeks was called the <a href="/wiki/Erythraean_Sea" title="Erythraean Sea">Erythraean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Periplus_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Periplus-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="982" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg/200px-Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg/300px-Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg/400px-Indian_Ocean_surface.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The ocean-floor of the Indian Ocean is divided by spreading ridges and crisscrossed by aseismic structures</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3718" data-file-width="3718" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg/200px-Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg/300px-Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg/400px-Blue_Marble_Eastern_Hemisphere.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A composite satellite image centred on the Indian Ocean</div></div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_and_data">Extent and data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Extent and data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="330" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Indian_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png/310px-Indian_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Indian_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png 1.5x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Ocean_-_en_IHO.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Indian Ocean, according to the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> (blue area), and as defined by the IHO (black outline - excluding marginal waterbodies).</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Indian_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">borders of the Indian Ocean</a>, as delineated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> in 1953 included the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> but not the marginal seas along the northern rim, but in 2000 the IHO delimited the Southern Ocean separately, which removed waters south of 60°S from the Indian Ocean but included the northern marginal seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO-2002_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-2002-11">[11]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meridional" title="Meridional">Meridionally</a>, the Indian Ocean is delimited from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> by the <a href="/wiki/20th_meridian_east" title="20th meridian east">20° east meridian</a>, running south from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> by the meridian of 146°49'E, running south from the southernmost point of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>. The northernmost extent of the Indian Ocean (including marginal seas) is approximately <a href="/wiki/30th_parallel_north" title="30th parallel north">30° north</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO-2002_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-2002-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian Ocean covers 70,560,000 km<sup>2</sup> (27,240,000 sq mi), including the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the Persian Gulf but excluding the Southern Ocean, or 19.5% of the world's oceans; its volume is 264,000,000 km<sup>3</sup> (63,000,000 cu mi) or 19.8% of the world's oceans' volume; it has an average depth of 3,741 m (12,274 ft) and a maximum depth of 7,906 m (25,938 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA-volume_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-volume-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>All of the Indian Ocean is in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a> and the centre of the Eastern Hemisphere, the <a href="/wiki/90th_meridian_east" title="90th meridian east">90th meridian east</a>, passes through the <a href="/wiki/Ninety_East_Ridge" title="Ninety East Ridge">Ninety East Ridge</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coasts_and_shelves">Coasts and shelves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Coasts and shelves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In contrast to the Atlantic and Pacific, the Indian Ocean is enclosed by major landmasses and an archipelago on three sides and does not stretch from pole to pole, and can be likened to an embayed ocean. It is centered on the Indian Peninsula. Although this subcontinent has played a significant role in its history, the Indian Ocean has foremostly been a cosmopolitan stage, interlinking diverse regions by innovations, trade, and religion since early in human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prange-2008-p1382_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prange-2008-p1382-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_margin" title="Active margin">active margins</a> of the Indian Ocean have an average depth (land to shelf break<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>) of 19 ± 0.61 km (11.81 ± 0.38 mi) with a maximum depth of 175 km (109 mi). The <a href="/wiki/Passive_margin" title="Passive margin">passive margins</a> have an average depth of 47.6 ± 0.8 km (29.58 ± 0.50 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
The average width of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">slopes</a> of the continental shelves are 50.4–52.4 km (31.3–32.6 mi) for active and passive margins respectively, with a maximum depth of 205.3–255.2 km (127.6–158.6 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In correspondence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shelf_break" title="Shelf break">Shelf break</a>, also known as Hinge zone, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bouguer_gravity" title="Bouguer gravity">Bouguer gravity</a> ranges from 0 to 30 <a href="/wiki/Gal_(unit)" title="Gal (unit)">mGals</a> that is unusual for a continental region of around 16 km thick sediments. It has been hypothesized that the "Hinge zone may represent the relict of continental and proto-oceanic crustal boundary formed during the rifting of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> from <a href="/wiki/Antarctica#Geography" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1365-246X_208_3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-1365-246X_208_3-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Australia, Indonesia, and India are the three countries with the longest shorelines and <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zones</a>. The continental shelf makes up 15% of the Indian Ocean.
More than two billion people live in countries bordering the Indian Ocean, compared to 1.7 billion for the Atlantic and 2.7 billion for the Pacific (some countries border more than one ocean).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeeIrv-2005_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-KeeIrv-2005-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers">Rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Indian Ocean <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> covers 21,100,000 km<sup>2</sup> (8,100,000 sq mi), virtually identical to that of the Pacific Ocean and half that of the Atlantic basin, or 30% of its ocean surface (compared to 15% for the Pacific). The Indian Ocean drainage basin is divided into roughly 800 individual basins, half that of the Pacific, of which 50% are located in Asia, 30% in Africa, and 20% in Australasia. The rivers of the Indian Ocean are shorter on average (740 km (460 mi)) than those of the other major oceans. The largest rivers are (<a href="/wiki/Strahler_number" title="Strahler number">order 5</a>) the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_River" title="Ganges River">Ganges</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jubba_River" title="Jubba River">Jubba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Murray_River" title="Murray River">Murray</a> rivers and (order 4) the <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wadi_Ad_Dawasir" title="Wadi Ad Dawasir">Wadi Ad Dawasir</a> (a dried-out river system on the Arabian Peninsula) and <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo</a> rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
After the breakup of East <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> and the formation of the Himalayas, the Ganges-Brahmaputra rivers flow into the world's largest delta known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengal_delta" title="Bengal delta">Bengal delta</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunderbans" title="Sunderbans">Sunderbans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1365-246X_208_3_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-1365-246X_208_3-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marginal_seas">Marginal seas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Marginal seas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_seas" title="Marginal seas">Marginal seas</a>, gulfs, bays and straits of the Indian Ocean include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO-2002_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-2002-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along the east coast of Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a> separates <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> from mainland Africa, while the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Zanj" title="Sea of Zanj">Sea of Zanj</a> is located north of Madagascar.
</p><p>On the northern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a> is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> by the strait of <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a>. In the Gulf of Aden, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tadjoura" title="Gulf of Tadjoura">Gulf of Tadjoura</a> is located in Djibouti and the <a href="/wiki/Guardafui_Channel" title="Guardafui Channel">Guardafui Channel</a> separates Socotra island from the Horn of Africa. The northern end of the Red Sea terminates in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aqaba" title="Gulf of Aqaba">Gulf of Aqaba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Suez" title="Gulf of Suez">Gulf of Suez</a>. The Indian Ocean is artificially connected to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> without ship lock through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, which is accessible via the Red Sea.
The Arabian Sea is connected to the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Oman" title="Gulf of Oman">Gulf of Oman</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a>. In the Persian Gulf, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bahrain" title="Gulf of Bahrain">Gulf of Bahrain</a> separates Qatar from the Arabic Peninsula.
</p><p>Along the west coast of India, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Kutch" title="Gulf of Kutch">Gulf of Kutch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Khambat" title="Gulf of Khambat">Gulf of Khambat</a> are located in Gujarat in the northern end while the <a href="/wiki/Laccadive_Sea" title="Laccadive Sea">Laccadive Sea</a> separates the Maldives from the southern tip of India.
The <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> is off the east coast of India. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mannar" title="Gulf of Mannar">Gulf of Mannar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palk_Strait" title="Palk Strait">Palk Strait</a> separates Sri Lanka from India, while the <a href="/wiki/Adam%27s_Bridge" title="Adam's Bridge">Adam's Bridge</a> separates the two. The <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a> is located between the Bay of Bengal and the Andaman Islands.
</p><p>In Indonesia, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Indonesia#Indonesian_Seaway" title="Geology of Indonesia">Indonesian Seaway</a> is composed of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacca_Strait" title="Malacca Strait">Malacca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">Sunda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Straits</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Carpentaria" title="Gulf of Carpentaria">Gulf of Carpentaria</a> of located on the Australian north coast while the <a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a> constitutes a large part of its southern coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 28em;">

<li><a href="/wiki/Flores_Sea" title="Flores Sea">Flores Sea</a> - 240,000 km<sup>2</sup></li>

<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oman_Sea" title="Oman Sea">Oman Sea</a> - 181,000 km<sup>2</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a> - 45,926 km<sup>2</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aqaba" title="Gulf of Aqaba">Gulf of Aqaba</a> - 239 km<sup>2</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Khambhat" title="Gulf of Khambhat">Gulf of Khambhat</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Kutch" title="Gulf of Kutch">Gulf of Kutch</a></li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg/220px-Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg/330px-Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg/440px-Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indian_Ocean_Monsoon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During summer, warm continental masses draw moist air from the Indian Ocean hence producing heavy rainfall. The process is reversed during winter, resulting in dry conditions.</div></div></div>
<p>Several features make the Indian Ocean unique. It constitutes the core of the large-scale <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Warm_Pool" title="Tropical Warm Pool">Tropical Warm Pool</a> which, when interacting with the atmosphere, affects the climate both regionally and globally. Asia blocks heat export and prevents the ventilation of the Indian Ocean <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>. That continent also drives the Indian Ocean <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>, the strongest on Earth, which causes large-scale seasonal variations in ocean currents, including the reversal of the <a href="/wiki/Somali_Current" title="Somali Current">Somali Current</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indian_Monsoon_Current" title="Indian Monsoon Current">Indian Monsoon Current</a>. Because of the Indian Ocean <a href="/wiki/Walker_circulation" title="Walker circulation">Walker circulation</a> there are no continuous equatorial easterlies. <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">Upwelling</a> occurs near the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> and north of the trade winds in the Southern Hemisphere. The <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Throughflow" title="Indonesian Throughflow">Indonesian Throughflow</a> is a unique Equatorial connection to the Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The climate north of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> is affected by a <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> climate. Strong north-east winds blow from October until April; from May until October south and west winds prevail. In the Arabian Sea, the violent Monsoon brings rain to the Indian subcontinent. In the southern hemisphere, the winds are generally milder, but summer storms near Mauritius can be severe. When the monsoon winds change, cyclones sometimes strike the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-22">[22]</a></sup>
Some 80% of the total annual rainfall in India occurs during summer and the region is so dependent on this rainfall that many civilisations perished when the Monsoon failed in the past. The huge variability in the Indian Summer Monsoon has also occurred pre-historically, with a strong, wet phase 33,500–32,500 BP; a weak, dry phase 26,000–23,500 BC; and a very weak phase 17,000–15,000 BP,
corresponding to a series of dramatic global events: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%B8lling-Aller%C3%B8d_warming" title="Bølling-Allerød warming">Bølling-Allerød</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_event" title="Heinrich event">Heinrich</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">Younger Dryas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India,_Bangladesh,_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7200" data-file-width="5600" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg/220px-Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg/330px-Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg/440px-Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India%2C_Bangladesh%2C_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerosol_pollution_over_Northern_India,_Bangladesh,_and_Bay_of_Bengal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Air pollution in South Asia spread over the Bay of Bengal and beyond.</div></div></div>
<p>The Indian Ocean is the warmest ocean in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Long-term ocean temperature records show a rapid, continuous warming in the Indian Ocean, at about 1.2 °C (34.2 °F) (compared to 0.7 °C (33.3 °F) for the warm pool region) during 1901–2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[25]</a></sup> Research indicates that human induced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_warming" title="Greenhouse warming">greenhouse warming</a>, and changes in the frequency and magnitude of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> (or the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Dipole" title="Indian Ocean Dipole">Indian Ocean Dipole</a>), events are a trigger to this strong warming in the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>South of the Equator (20-5°S), the Indian Ocean is gaining heat from June to October, during the austral winter, while it is losing heat from November to March, during the austral summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Experiment" title="Indian Ocean Experiment">Indian Ocean Experiment</a> showed that fossil fuel and biomass burning in South and Southeast Asia caused air pollution (also known as the <a href="/wiki/Asian_brown_cloud" title="Asian brown cloud">Asian brown cloud</a>) that reach as far as the <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a> at 60°S. This pollution has implications on both a local and global scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanography">Oceanography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Oceanography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>40% of the sediment of the Indian Ocean is found in the Indus and Ganges fans. The oceanic basins adjacent to the continental slopes mostly contain terrigenous sediments. The ocean south of the <a href="/wiki/Polar_front" title="Polar front">polar front</a> (roughly <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50° south latitude</a>) is high in biologic productivity and dominated by non-stratified sediment composed mostly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siliceous" title="Siliceous">siliceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_sediment#Oozes" title="Pelagic sediment">oozes</a>. Near the three major mid-ocean ridges the ocean floor is relatively young and therefore bare of sediment, except for the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Indian_Ridge" title="Southwest Indian Ridge">Southwest Indian Ridge</a> due to its ultra-slow spreading rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ocean's <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">currents</a> are mainly controlled by the monsoon. Two large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyre" title="Gyre">gyres</a>, one in the northern hemisphere flowing clockwise and one south of the equator moving anticlockwise (including the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Current</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Return_Current" title="Agulhas Return Current">Agulhas Return Current</a>), constitute the dominant flow pattern. During the winter monsoon (November–February), however, circulation is reversed north of 30°S and winds are weakened during winter and the transitional periods between the monsoons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian Ocean contains the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submarine_fan" title="Submarine fan">submarine fans</a> of the world, the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Fan" title="Bengal Fan">Bengal Fan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indus_Fan" title="Indus Fan">Indus Fan</a>, and the largest areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_terrace" title="Marine terrace">slope terraces</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valleys</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inflow of deep water into the Indian Ocean is 11 <a href="/wiki/Sverdrup" title="Sverdrup">Sv</a>, most of which comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumpolar_Deep_Water" title="Circumpolar Deep Water">Circumpolar Deep Water</a> (CDW). The CDW enters the Indian Ocean through the <a href="/wiki/Crozet_Islands" title="Crozet Islands">Crozet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> basins and crosses the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Indian_Ridge" title="Southwest Indian Ridge">Southwest Indian Ridge</a> at 30°S. In the <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Basin" title="Mascarene Basin">Mascarene Basin</a> the CDW becomes a deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_boundary_current" title="Western boundary current">western boundary current</a> before it is met by a re-circulated branch of itself, the North Indian Deep Water. This mixed water partly flows north into the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Somali_Basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Somali Basin (page does not exist)">Somali Basin</a> whilst most of it flows clockwise in the Mascarene Basin where an oscillating flow is produced by <a href="/wiki/Rossby_wave#Oceanic_waves" title="Rossby wave">Rossby waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water circulation in the Indian Ocean is dominated by the Subtropical Anticyclonic Gyre, the eastern extension of which is blocked by the Southeast Indian Ridge and the 90°E Ridge. Madagascar and the Southwest Indian Ridge separate three cells south of Madagascar and off South Africa. <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> reaches into the Indian Ocean south of Africa at a depth of 2,000–3,000 m (6,600–9,800 ft) and flows north along the eastern continental slope of Africa. Deeper than NADW, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antarctic_Bottom_Water" title="Antarctic Bottom Water">Antarctic Bottom Water</a> flows from <a href="/wiki/Enderby_Plain" title="Enderby Plain">Enderby Basin</a> to <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Basin" title="Agulhas Basin">Agulhas Basin</a> across deep channels (&lt;4,000 m (13,000 ft)) in the Southwest Indian Ridge, from where it continues into the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Fracture_Zone" title="Prince Edward Fracture Zone">Prince Edward Fracture Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers-p5_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-p5-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>North of <a href="/wiki/20th_parallel_south" title="20th parallel south">20° south latitude</a> the minimum surface temperature is 22 °C (72 °F), exceeding 28 °C (82 °F) to the east. Southward of <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40° south latitude</a>, temperatures drop quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> contributes more than half (2,950 km<sup>3</sup> or 710 cu mi) of the <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff water</a> to the Indian Ocean. Mainly in summer, this runoff flows into the Arabian Sea but also south across the Equator where it mixes with fresher seawater from the <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Throughflow" title="Indonesian Throughflow">Indonesian Throughflow</a>. This mixed freshwater joins the <a href="/wiki/South_Equatorial_Current" title="South Equatorial Current">South Equatorial Current</a> in the southern tropical Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Salinity#Seawater" title="Salinity">Sea surface salinity</a> is highest (more than 36 <a href="/wiki/Salinity#PSU" title="Salinity">PSU</a>) in the Arabian Sea because evaporation exceeds precipitation there. In the Southeast Arabian Sea salinity drops to less than 34 PSU. It is the lowest (c. 33 PSU) in the Bay of Bengal because of river runoff and precipitation. The Indonesian Throughflow and precipitation results in lower salinity (34 PSU) along the Sumatran west coast. Monsoonal variation results in eastward transportation of saltier water from the Arabian Sea to the Bay of Bengal from June to September and in westerly transport by the East India Coastal Current to the Arabian Sea from January to April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_garbage_patch" title="Indian Ocean garbage patch">Indian Ocean garbage patch</a> was discovered in 2010 covering at least 5 million square kilometres (1.9 million square miles). Riding the southern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Gyre" title="Indian Ocean Gyre">Indian Ocean Gyre</a>, this vortex of <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic garbage</a> constantly circulates the ocean from Australia to Africa, down the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a>, and back to Australia in a period of six years, except for debris that gets indefinitely stuck in the centre of the gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
The garbage patch in the Indian Ocean will, according to a 2012 study, decrease in size after several decades to vanish completely over centuries. Over several millennia, however, the global system of garbage patches will accumulate in the North Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidromes</a> of opposite rotation in the Indian Ocean, probably caused by <a href="/wiki/Rossby_wave" title="Rossby wave">Rossby wave</a> propagation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">Icebergs</a> drift as far north as <a href="/wiki/55th_parallel_south" title="55th parallel south">55° south latitude</a>, similar to the Pacific but less than in the Atlantic where icebergs reach up to 45°S. The volume of iceberg loss in the Indian Ocean between 2004 and 2012 was 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigatonne" title="Gigatonne">Gt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1960s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_climate_change" title="Anthropogenic climate change">anthropogenic warming</a> of the global ocean combined with contributions of freshwater from retreating land ice causes a global rise in sea level. Sea level increases in the Indian Ocean too, except in the south tropical Indian Ocean where it decreases, a pattern most likely caused by rising levels of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_life">Marine life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Marine life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:592px;max-width:592px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" class="tsingle" style="width:187px;max-width:187px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1530" data-file-width="2040" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg/185px-Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg/278px-Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg/370px-Vagues_et_manchots_%C3%A0_l%27assaut_de_la_plage.jpg 2x" width="185"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">A dolphin off Western Australia and a swarm of <a href="/wiki/Acanthurus_leucosternon" title="Acanthurus leucosternon">surgeonfish</a> near Maldives Islands represents the well-known, exotic fauna of the warmer parts of the Indian Ocean. King Penguins on a beach in the <a href="/wiki/Crozet_Islands" title="Crozet Islands">Crozet Archipelago</a> near Antarctica attract fewer tourists.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> oceans, the western Indian Ocean hosts one of the largest concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> blooms in summer, due to the strong <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds. The monsoonal wind forcing leads to a strong coastal and open ocean <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a>, which introduces nutrients into the upper zones where sufficient light is available for <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> and phytoplankton production. These phytoplankton blooms support the marine ecosystem, as the base of the marine food web, and eventually the larger fish species. The Indian Ocean accounts for the second-largest share of the most economically valuable <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> catch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Its fish are of great and growing importance to the bordering countries for domestic consumption and export. Fishing fleets from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> also exploit the Indian Ocean, mainly for <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> and tuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-2018_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-2018-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research indicates that increasing ocean temperatures are taking a toll on the marine ecosystem. A study on the phytoplankton changes in the Indian Ocean indicates a decline of up to 20% in the marine plankton in the Indian Ocean, during the past six decades. The tuna catch rates have also declined 50–90% during the past half-century, mostly due to increased industrial fisheries, with the ocean warming adding further stress to the fish species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Endangered and vulnerable marine mammals and turtles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>80% of the Indian Ocean is open ocean and includes nine <a href="/wiki/Large_marine_ecosystem" title="Large marine ecosystem">large marine ecosystems</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Current</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Somali_Coastal_Current&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Somali Coastal Current (page does not exist)">Somali Coastal Current</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=West_Central_Australian_Shelf&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="West Central Australian Shelf (page does not exist)">West Central Australian Shelf</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Northwest_Australian_Shelf&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Northwest Australian Shelf (page does not exist)">Northwest Australian Shelf</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Southwest_Australian_Shelf&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Southwest Australian Shelf (page does not exist)">Southwest Australian Shelf</a>. Coral reefs cover c. 200,000 km<sup>2</sup> (77,000 sq mi). The coasts of the Indian Ocean includes beaches and intertidal zones covering 3,000 km<sup>2</sup> (1,200 sq mi) and 246 larger <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>. <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">Upwelling</a> areas are small but important. The hypersaline <a href="/wiki/Saltern" title="Saltern">salterns</a> in India covers between 5,000–10,000 km<sup>2</sup> (1,900–3,900 sq mi) and species adapted for this environment, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Artemia_salina" title="Artemia salina">Artemia salina</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dunaliella_salina" title="Dunaliella salina">Dunaliella salina</a></i>, are important to bird life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:228px;max-width:228px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:169px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Padadita_Beach,_Waingapu_18.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg/226px-Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg/339px-Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg/452px-Padadita_Beach%2C_Waingapu_18.jpg 2x" width="226"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:160px;max-width:160px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:169px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coelacanth1.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1857" data-file-width="1731" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Coelacanth1.JPG/158px-Coelacanth1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Coelacanth1.JPG/237px-Coelacanth1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Coelacanth1.JPG/316px-Coelacanth1.JPG 2x" width="158"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: Mangroves (here in East Nusa Tenggara, Indonesia) are the only tropical to subtropical forests adapted for a coastal environment.  From their origin on the coasts of the Indo-Malaysian region, they have reached a global distribution.<br/>Right: The coelacanth (here a model from Oxford), thought extinct for million years, was rediscovered in the 20th century.  The Indian Ocean species is blue whereas the Indonesian species is brown.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Coral reefs, sea grass beds, and mangrove forests are the most productive ecosystems of the Indian Ocean — coastal areas produce 20 tones per square kilometre of fish. These areas, however, are also being urbanised with populations often exceeding several thousand people per square kilometre and fishing techniques become more effective and often destructive beyond sustainable levels while the increase in sea surface temperature spreads coral bleaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">Mangroves</a> covers 80,984 km<sup>2</sup> (31,268 sq mi) in the Indian Ocean region, or almost half of the world's mangrove habitat, of which 42,500 km<sup>2</sup> (16,400 sq mi) is located in Indonesia, or 50% of mangroves in the Indian Ocean. Mangroves originated in the Indian Ocean region and have adapted to a wide range of its habitats but it is also where it suffers its biggest loss of habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016 six new animal species were identified at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a> in the Southwest Indian Ridge: a "Hoff" crab, a "giant peltospirid" snail, a whelk-like snail, a limpet, a scaleworm and a polychaete worm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/West_Indian_Ocean_coelacanth" title="West Indian Ocean coelacanth">West Indian Ocean coelacanth</a> was discovered in the Indian Ocean off South Africa in the 1930s and in the late 1990s another species, the <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_coelacanth" title="Indonesian coelacanth">Indonesian coelacanth</a>, was discovered off <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulawesi_Island" title="Sulawesi Island">Sulawesi Island</a>, Indonesia. Most extant coelacanths have been found in the Comoros. Although both species represent an order of <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fishes</a> known from the Early Devonian (410 <abbr title="million years ago">mya</abbr>) and though extinct 66 mya, they are morphologically distinct from their Devonian ancestors. Over millions of years, coelacanths evolved to inhabit different environments — lungs adapted for shallow, brackish waters evolved into gills adapted for deep marine waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Of Earth's 36 <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">biodiversity hotspot</a> nine (or 25%) are located on the margins of the Indian Ocean.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vogeleiland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Vogeleiland.jpg/220px-Vogeleiland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Vogeleiland.jpg/330px-Vogeleiland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Vogeleiland.jpg/440px-Vogeleiland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vogeleiland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Madagascar's <a href="/wiki/Elephant_bird" title="Elephant bird">Elephant bird</a>, Mauritius's <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">Dodo</a> bird and ostrich (from left to right)</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Madagascar and the islands of the western Indian Ocean (Comoros, Réunion, Mauritius, Rodrigues, the Seychelles, and Socotra), includes 13,000 (11,600 endemic) species of plants; 313 (183) birds; reptiles 381 (367); 164 (97) freshwater fishes; 250 (249) amphibians; and 200 (192) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The origin of this diversity is debated; the break-up of Gondwana can explain vicariance older than 100 mya, but the diversity on the younger, smaller islands must have required a Cenozoic dispersal from the rims of the Indian Ocean to the islands. A "reverse colonisation", from islands to continents, apparently occurred more recently; the <a href="/wiki/Chameleon" title="Chameleon">chameleons</a>, for example, first diversified on Madagascar and then colonised Africa. Several species on the islands of the Indian Ocean are textbook cases of evolutionary processes; the <a href="/wiki/Dung_beetle" title="Dung beetle">dung beetles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_gecko" title="Day gecko">day geckos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemurs</a> are all examples of <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
Many bones (250 bones per square metre) of recently extinct vertebrates have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Mare_aux_Songes" title="Mare aux Songes">Mare aux Songes</a> swamp in Mauritius, including bones of the <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">Dodo</a> bird (<i>Raphus cucullatus</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Cylindraspis" title="Cylindraspis">Cylindraspis</a></i> giant tortoise. An analysis of these remains suggests a process of aridification began in the southwest Indian Ocean began around 4,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Maputaland-Pondoland-Albany_Hotspot" title="Maputaland-Pondoland-Albany Hotspot">Maputaland-Pondoland-Albany</a> (MPA); 8,100 (1,900 endemic) species of plants; 541 (0) birds; 205 (36) reptiles; 73 (20) freshwater fishes; 73 (11) amphibians; and 197 (3) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Mammalian megafauna once widespread in the MPA was driven to near extinction in the early 20th century. Some species have been successfully recovered since then — the population of <a href="/wiki/White_rhinoceros" title="White rhinoceros">white rhinoceros</a> (<i>Ceratotherium simum simum</i>) increased from less than 20 individuals in 1895 to more than 17,000 as of 2013. Other species are still dependent of fenced areas and management programs, including <a href="/wiki/Black_rhinoceros" title="Black rhinoceros">black rhinoceros</a> (<i>Diceros bicornis minor</i>), <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dog</a> (<i>Lycaon pictus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a> (<i>Acynonix jubatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> (<i>Loxodonta africana</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a> (<i>Panthera leo</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Coastal_forests_of_eastern_Africa" title="Coastal forests of eastern Africa">Coastal forests of eastern Africa</a>; 4,000 (1,750 endemic) species of plants; 636 (12) birds; 250 (54) reptiles; 219 (32) freshwater fishes; 95 (10) amphibians; and 236 (7) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>This biodiversity hotspot (and namesake ecoregion and "Endemic Bird Area") is a patchwork of small forested areas, often with a unique assemblage of species within each, located within 200 km (120 mi) from the coast and covering a total area of c. 6,200 km<sup>2</sup> (2,400 sq mi). It also encompasses coastal islands, including Zanzibar and Pemba, and Mafia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>; 5,000 (2,750 endemic) species of plants; 704 (25) birds; 284 (93) reptiles; 100 (10) freshwater fishes; 30 (6) amphibians; and 189 (18) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vaavu_Atoll,_Maldives_-_panoramio_(20).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1671" data-file-width="2972" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg/220px-Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg/330px-Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg/440px-Vaavu_Atoll%2C_Maldives_-_panoramio_%2820%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vaavu_Atoll,_Maldives_-_panoramio_(20).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">Coral reefs</a> of the Maldives</div></div></div></li></ul>
<p>This area, one of the only two hotspots that are entirely arid, includes the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift valley</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Socotra" title="Socotra">Socotra</a> islands, as well as some small islands in the Red Sea and areas on the southern Arabic Peninsula. Endemic and threatened mammals include the <a href="/wiki/Dibatag" title="Dibatag">dibatag</a> (<i>Ammodorcas clarkei</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Speke%27s_gazelle" title="Speke's gazelle">Speke's gazelle</a> (<i>Gazella spekei</i>); the <a href="/wiki/Somali_wild_ass" title="Somali wild ass">Somali wild ass</a> (<i>Equus africanus somaliensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Hamadryas_baboon" title="Hamadryas baboon">hamadryas baboon</a> (<i>Papio hamadryas</i>). It also contains many reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
In Somalia, the centre of the 1,500,000 km<sup>2</sup> (580,000 sq mi) hotspot, the landscape is dominated by <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">Acacia</a>-<a href="/wiki/Commiphora" title="Commiphora">Commiphora</a> deciduous bushland, but also includes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeheb_nut" title="Yeheb nut">Yeheb nut</a> (<i>Cordeauxia edulus</i>) and species discovered more recently such as the Somali <a href="/wiki/Cyclamen" title="Cyclamen">cyclamen</a> (<i>Cyclamen somalense</i>), the only cyclamen outside the Mediterranean. <a href="/wiki/Warsangli_linnet" title="Warsangli linnet">Warsangli linnet</a> (<i>Carduelis johannis</i>) is an endemic bird found only in northern Somalia. An unstable political situation and mismanagement has resulted in overgrazing which has produced one of the most degraded hotspots where only c. 5 % of the original habitat remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Western_Ghats" title="Western Ghats">Western Ghats</a>–<a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>; 5,916 (3,049 endemic) species of plants; 457 (35) birds; 265 (176) reptiles; 191 (139) freshwater fishes; 204 (156) amphibians; and 143 (27) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Encompassing the west coast of India and Sri Lanka, until c. 10,000 years ago a landbridge connected Sri Lanka to the Indian Subcontinent, hence this region shares a common community of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Burma" title="Indo-Burma">Indo-Burma</a>; 13.500 (7,000 endemic) species of plants; 1,277 (73) birds; 518 (204) reptiles; 1,262 (553) freshwater fishes; 328 (193) amphibians; and 401 (100) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/%D0%9E._%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0_-_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B5_%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%88%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%85_%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B2.jpg/330px-%D0%9E._%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0_-_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B5_%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%88%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%85_%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B2.jpg
