//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/%D0%9E._%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0_-_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B5_%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%88%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%85_%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B2.jpg/440px-%D0%9E._%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0_-_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B5_%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%88%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%85_%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9E._%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B4%D0%B0%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0_-_%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%BD%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%8B%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB_%D0%B2_%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%85%D0%B8%D0%BF%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B5_%D0%A1%D0%B5%D0%B9%D1%88%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D1%85_%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aldabra_giant_tortoise" title="Aldabra giant tortoise">Aldabra giant tortoise</a> from the islands of the <a href="/wiki/Aldabra" title="Aldabra">Aldabra</a> Atoll in the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a></div></div></div></li></ul>
<p>Indo-Burma encompasses a series of mountain ranges, five of Asia's largest river systems, and a wide range of habitats. The region has a long and complex geological history, and long periods <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">rising sea levels</a> and glaciations have isolated ecosystems and thus promoted a high degree of endemism and <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>. The region includes two centres of endemism: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annamite_Mountains" title="Annamite Mountains">Annamite Mountains</a> and the northern highlands on the China-Vietnam border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
Several distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floristic_region" title="Floristic region">floristic regions</a>, the Indian, Malesian, Sino-Himalayan, and Indochinese regions, meet in a unique way in Indo-Burma and the hotspot contains an estimated 15,000–25,000 species of vascular plants, many of them endemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sundaland" title="Sundaland">Sundaland</a>; 25,000 (15,000 endemic) species of plants; 771 (146) birds; 449 (244) reptiles; 950 (350) freshwater fishes; 258 (210) amphibians; and 397 (219) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Sundaland encompasses 17,000 islands of which Borneo and Sumatra are the largest. Endangered mammals include the <a href="/wiki/Bornean_orangutan" title="Bornean orangutan">Bornean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatran_orangutan" title="Sumatran orangutan">Sumatran orangutans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Proboscis_monkey" title="Proboscis monkey">proboscis monkey</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Javan_rhinoceros" title="Javan rhinoceros">Javan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatran_rhinoceros" title="Sumatran rhinoceros">Sumatran rhinoceroses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a>; 10,000 (1,500 endemic) species of plants; 650 (265) birds; 222 (99) reptiles; 250 (50) freshwater fishes; 49 (33) amphibians; and 244 (144) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_Australia_(ecoregion)" title="Southwest Australia (ecoregion)">Southwest Australia</a>; 5,571 (2,948 endemic) species of plants; 285 (10) birds; 177 (27) reptiles; 20 (10) freshwater fishes; 32 (22) amphibians; and 55 (13) mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitter-2011-p12_48-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mitter-2011-p12-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Stretching from <a href="/wiki/Shark_Bay" title="Shark Bay">Shark Bay</a> to <a href="/wiki/Israelite_Bay" title="Israelite Bay">Israelite Bay</a> and isolated by the arid <a href="/wiki/Nullarbor_Plain" title="Nullarbor Plain">Nullarbor Plain</a>, the southwestern corner of Australia is a floristic region with a stable climate in which one of the world's largest floral biodiversity and an 80% endemism has evolved. From June to September it is an explosion of colours and the Wildflower Festival in Perth in September attracts more than half a million visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="791" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/180px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/270px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/360px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/180px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/270px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/360px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: The oldest ocean floor of the Indian Ocean formed c. 150 Ma when the Indian Subcontinent and Madagascar broke-up from Africa.  Right: The India–Asia collision c. 40 Ma completed the closure of the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> (grey areas north of India).  Geologically, the Indian Ocean is the ocean floor that opened up south of India.</div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main category: <a href="/wiki/Category:Landforms_of_the_Indian_Ocean" title="Category:Landforms of the Indian Ocean">Landforms of the Indian Ocean</a></div>
<p>As the youngest of the major oceans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> the Indian Ocean has active spreading ridges that are part of the worldwide system of <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a>. In the Indian Ocean these spreading ridges meet at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodrigues_Triple_Point" title="Rodrigues Triple Point">Rodrigues Triple Point</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Central_Indian_Ridge" title="Central Indian Ridge">Central Indian Ridge</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Carlsberg_Ridge" title="Carlsberg Ridge">Carlsberg Ridge</a>, separating the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African Plate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Indian_Ridge" title="Southwest Indian Ridge">Southwest Indian Ridge</a> separating the African Plate from the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Indian_Ridge" title="Southeast Indian Ridge">Southeast Indian Ridge</a> separating the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian Plate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a>. The Central Indian Ridge is intercepted by the <a href="/wiki/Owen_Fracture_Zone" title="Owen Fracture Zone">Owen Fracture Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-60">[60]</a></sup>
Since the late 1990s, however, it has become clear that this traditional definition of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a> cannot be correct; it consists of three plates — the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Capricorn_Plate" title="Capricorn Plate">Capricorn Plate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian Plate</a> — separated by diffuse boundary zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
Since 20 Ma the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African Plate</a> is being divided by the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a> System into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nubian_Plate" title="Nubian Plate">Nubian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somali_Plate" title="Somali Plate">Somalia</a> plates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are only two trenches in the Indian Ocean: the 6,000 km (3,700 mi)-long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_Trench" title="Java Trench">Java Trench</a> between Java and the Sunda Trench and the 900 km (560 mi)-long <a href="/wiki/Makran_Trench" title="Makran Trench">Makran Trench</a> south of Iran and Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>A series of ridges and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seamount_chain" title="Seamount chain">seamount chains</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspots</a> pass over the Indian Ocean. The <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union_hotspot" title="Réunion hotspot">Réunion hotspot</a> (active 70–40 million years ago) connects <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="Réunion">Réunion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Plateau" title="Mascarene Plateau">Mascarene Plateau</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chagos-Laccadive_Ridge" title="Chagos-Laccadive Ridge">Chagos-Laccadive Ridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> in north-western India; the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_hotspot" title="Kerguelen hotspot">Kerguelen hotspot</a> (100–35 million years ago) connects the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Islands" title="Kerguelen Islands">Kerguelen Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ninety_East_Ridge" title="Ninety East Ridge">Ninety East Ridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rajmahal_Traps" title="Rajmahal Traps">Rajmahal Traps</a> in north-eastern India; the Marion hotspot (100–70 million years ago) possibly connects <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Islands" title="Prince Edward Islands">Prince Edward Islands</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Five_East_Ridge" title="Eighty Five East Ridge">Eighty Five East Ridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> These hotspot tracks have been broken by the still active spreading ridges mentioned above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are fewer seamounts in the Indian Ocean than in the Atlantic and Pacific. These are typically deeper than 3,000 m (9,800 ft) and located north of 55°S and west of 80°E. Most originated at spreading ridges but some are now located in basins far away from these ridges. The ridges of the Indian Ocean form ranges of seamounts, sometimes very long, including the <a href="/wiki/Carlsberg_Ridge" title="Carlsberg Ridge">Carlsberg Ridge</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar_Ridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Madagascar Ridge (page does not exist)">Madagascar Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Indian_Ridge" title="Central Indian Ridge">Central Indian Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Indian_Ridge" title="Southwest Indian Ridge">Southwest Indian Ridge</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chagos-Laccadive_Ridge" title="Chagos-Laccadive Ridge">Chagos-Laccadive Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Five_East_Ridge" title="Eighty Five East Ridge">85°E Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ninety_East_Ridge" title="Ninety East Ridge">90°E Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Indian_Ridge" title="Southeast Indian Ridge">Southeast Indian Ridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broken_Ridge" title="Broken Ridge">Broken Ridge</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=East_Indiaman_Ridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="East Indiaman Ridge (page does not exist)">East Indiaman Ridge</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Plateau" title="Agulhas Plateau">Agulhas Plateau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Plateau" title="Mascarene Plateau">Mascarene Plateau</a> are the two major shallow areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers-p5_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-p5-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The opening of the Indian Ocean began <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 156 <abbr title="million year ago">Ma</abbr> when Africa separated from East <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>. The Indian Subcontinent began to separate from Australia-Antarctica 135–125 Ma and as the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> north of India began to close 118–84 Ma the Indian Ocean opened behind it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Indian Ocean, together with the Mediterranean, has connected people since ancient times, whereas the Atlantic and Pacific have had the roles of barriers or <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_incognita" title="Terra incognita">mare incognitum</a></i>. The written history of the Indian Ocean, however, has been <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a> and largely dependent on the availability of written sources from the colonial era. This history is often divided into an ancient period followed by an Islamic period; the subsequent periods are often subdivided into <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>A concept of an "Indian Ocean World" (IOW), similar to that of the "<a href="/wiki/Atlantic_World" title="Atlantic World">Atlantic World</a>", exists but emerged much more recently and is not well established. The IOW is, nevertheless, sometimes referred to as the "first global economy" and was based on the monsoon which linked Asia, China, India, and Mesopotamia. It developed independently from the European global trade in the Mediterranean and Atlantic and remained largely independent from them until European 19th-century colonial dominance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The diverse history of the Indian Ocean is a unique mix of cultures, ethnic groups, natural resources, and shipping routes. It grew in importance beginning in the 1960s and 1970s and, after the Cold War, it has undergone periods of political instability, most recently with the emergence of India and China as regional powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_settlements">First settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg/220px-Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg/330px-Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg/440px-Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peopling_of_eurasia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>According to the <i>Coastal hypothesis</i>, modern humans spread from Africa along the northern rim of the Indian Ocean.</div></div></div>
<p>Pleistocene fossils of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> and other pre-<i>H. sapiens</i> hominid fossils, similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">H. heidelbergensis</a></i> in Europe, have been found in India. According to the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a>, a supereruption c. 74,000 years ago at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Lake Toba</a>, Sumatra, covered India with volcanic ashes and wiped out one or more lineages of such archaic humans in India and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans"><i>Out of Africa</i></a> theory states that <i>Homo sapiens</i> spread from Africa into mainland Eurasia. The more recent <i><a href="/wiki/Southern_Dispersal" title="Southern Dispersal">Southern Dispersal</a></i> or <i>Coastal hypothesis</i> instead advocates that modern humans spread along the coasts of the Arabic Peninsula and southern Asia. This hypothesis is supported by <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mtDNA</a> research which reveals a rapid dispersal event during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> (11,000 years ago). This coastal dispersal, however, began in East Africa 75,000 years ago and occurred intermittently from estuary to estuary along the northern perimeter of the Indian Ocean at a rate of 0.7–4.0 km (0.43–2.49 mi) per year. It eventually resulted in modern humans migrating from <a href="/wiki/Sundaland" title="Sundaland">Sunda</a> over <a href="/wiki/Wallacea" title="Wallacea">Wallacea</a> to <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Sahul</a> (Southeast Asia to Australia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
Since then, waves of migration have resettled people and, clearly, the Indian Ocean littoral had been inhabited long before the first civilisations emerged. 5000–6000 years ago six distinct cultural centres had evolved around the Indian Ocean: East Africa, the Middle East, the Indian Subcontinent, South East Asia, the Malay World, and Australia; each interlinked to its neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food globalisation began on the Indian Ocean littoral c. 4.000 years ago. Five African crops — <a href="/wiki/Sorghum_bicolor" title="Sorghum bicolor">sorghum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennisetum_glaucum" title="Pennisetum glaucum">pearl millet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleusine_coracana" title="Eleusine coracana">finger millet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vigna_unguiculata" title="Vigna unguiculata">cowpea</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lablab_purpureus" title="Lablab purpureus">hyacinth bean</a> — somehow found their way to <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> in India during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Harappan" title="Late Harappan">Late Harappan</a> (2000–1700 BCE). Gujarati merchants evolved into the first explorers of the Indian Ocean as they traded African goods such as ivory, tortoise shells, and slaves. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panicum_miliaceum" title="Panicum miliaceum">Broomcorn millet</a> found its way from Central Asia to Africa, together with chicken and <a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a> cattle, although the exact timing is disputed. Around 2000 BCE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piper_nigrum" title="Piper nigrum">black pepper</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesamum_indicum" title="Sesamum indicum">sesame</a>, both native to Asia, appear in Egypt, albeit in small quantities. Around the same time the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rattus_rattus" title="Rattus rattus">black rat</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus_musculus" title="Mus musculus">house mouse</a> emigrate from Asia to Egypt. Banana reached Africa around 3000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>At least eleven prehistoric tsunamis have struck the Indian Ocean coast of Indonesia between 7400 and 2900 years ago. Analysing sand beds in caves in the Aceh region, scientists concluded that the intervals between these tsunamis have varied from series of minor tsunamis over a century to dormant periods of more than 2000 years preceding megathrusts in the Sunda Trench. Although the risk for future tsunamis is high, a major megathrust such as the one in 2004 is likely to be followed by a long dormant period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>A group of scientists have argued that two large-scale impact events have occurred in the Indian Ocean: the <a href="/wiki/Burckle_Crater" title="Burckle Crater">Burckle Crater</a> in the southern Indian Ocean in 2800 BCE and the Kanmare and Tabban craters in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Carpentaria" title="Gulf of Carpentaria">Gulf of Carpentaria</a> in northern Australia in 536 CE. Evidences for these impacts, the team argue, are micro-ejecta and <a href="/wiki/Chevron_(land_form)" title="Chevron (land form)">Chevron</a> <a href="/wiki/Dune" title="Dune">dunes</a> in southern Madagascar and in the Australian gulf. Geological evidences suggest the tsunamis caused by these impacts reached 205 m (673 ft) above sea level and 45 km (28 mi) inland. The impact events must have disrupted human settlements and perhaps even contributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_weather_events_of_535%E2%80%93536" title="Extreme weather events of 535–536">major climate changes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The history of the Indian Ocean is marked by maritime trade; cultural and commercial exchange probably date back at least seven thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-73">[73]</a></sup> Human culture spread early on the shores of the Indian Ocean and was always linked to the cultures of the Mediterranean and the Persian Gulf. Before c. 2000 BCE, however, cultures on its shores were only loosely tied to each other; bronze, for example, was developed in Mesopotamia c. 3000 BCE but remained uncommon in Egypt before 1800 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
During this period, independent, short-distance oversea communications along its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> margins evolved into an all-embracing network. The début of this network was not the achievement of a centralised or advanced civilisation but of local and regional exchange in the Persian Gulf, the Red Sea, and the Arabian Sea. Sherds of <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid</a> (2500–500 BCE) pottery have been found in the western Gulf at <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>; traces of exchange between this trading centre and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> traded grain, pottery, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> (used for <a href="/wiki/Reed_boat" title="Reed boat">reed boats</a>) for copper, stone, timber, tin, dates, onions, and pearls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp19-22_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp19-22-75">[75]</a></sup>
Coast-bound vessels transported goods between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> (2600–1900 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>) in the Indian subcontinent (modern-day Pakistan and Northwest India) and the Persian Gulf and Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="2895" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/220px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/330px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/440px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade#Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" title="Indian Ocean trade">Austronesian maritime trade network</a> was the first trade routes in the Indian Ocean.</div></div></div>
<p>The Red Sea, one of the main trade routes in Antiquity, was explored by <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">Egyptians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> during the last two millennia BCE. In the 6th century, BCE Greek explorer <a href="/wiki/Scylax_of_Caryanda" title="Scylax of Caryanda">Scylax of Caryanda</a> made a journey to India, working for the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius</a>, and his now-lost account put the Indian Ocean on the maps of Greek geographers. The Greeks began to explore the Indian Ocean following the conquests of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, who ordered a circumnavigation of the Arabian Peninsula in 323 BCE. During the two centuries that followed the reports of the explorers of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> resulted in the best maps of the region until the Portuguese era many centuries later. The main interest in the region for the Ptolemies was not commercial but military; they explored Africa to hunt for <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rub%27_al_Khali" title="Rub' al Khali">Rub' al Khali</a> desert isolates the southern parts of the Arabic Peninsula and the Indian Ocean from the Arabic world. This encouraged the development of maritime trade in the region linking the Red Sea and the Persian Gulf to East Africa and India. The <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> (from <i>mawsim</i>, the Arabic word for season), however, was used by sailors long before being "discovered" by Hippalus in the 1st century. Indian wood have been found in Sumerian cities, there is evidence of Akkad coastal trade in the region, and contacts between India and the Red Sea dates back to 2300 B.C. The archipelagoes of the central Indian Ocean, the Laccadive and Maldive islands, were probably populated during the 2nd century B.C. from the Indian mainland. They appear in written history in the account of merchant <a href="/wiki/Sulaiman_al-Tajir" title="Sulaiman al-Tajir">Sulaiman al-Tajir</a> in the 9th century but the treacherous reefs of the islands were most likely cursed by the sailors of Aden long before the islands were even settled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indo-Roman_trade.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1413" data-file-width="2499" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Indo-Roman_trade.jpg/220px-Indo-Roman_trade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Indo-Roman_trade.jpg/330px-Indo-Roman_trade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Indo-Roman_trade.jpg/440px-Indo-Roman_trade.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indo-Roman_trade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Greco-Roman trade</a> with ancient India according to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periplus_Maris_Erythraei" title="Periplus Maris Erythraei">Periplus Maris Erythraei</a></i> 1st century CE</div></div></div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandrian</a> guide to the world beyond the Red Sea — including Africa and India — from the first century CE, not only gives insights into trade in the region but also shows that Roman and Greek sailors had already gained knowledge about the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-73">[73]</a></sup> The contemporaneous settlement of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_people" title="Austronesian people">Austronesian</a> sailors shows that the littoral margins of the Indian Ocean were being both well-populated and regularly traversed at least by this time. Albeit the monsoon must have been common knowledge in the Indian Ocean for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian Ocean's relatively calmer waters opened the areas bordering it to trade earlier than the Atlantic or Pacific oceans. The powerful monsoons also meant ships could easily sail west early in the season, then wait a few months and return eastwards. This allowed ancient Indonesian peoples to cross the Indian Ocean to settle in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> around 1 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2nd or 1st century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cyzicus" title="Eudoxus of Cyzicus">Eudoxus of Cyzicus</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a> to cross the Indian Ocean. The probably fictitious sailor <a href="/wiki/Hippalus" title="Hippalus">Hippalus</a> is said to have learnt the direct route from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> to India around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> During the 1st and 2nd centuries AD intensive <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">trade relations</a> developed between <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamil_people" title="Tamil people">Tamil kingdoms</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chera_Dynasty" title="Chera Dynasty">Cheras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chola_Dynasty" title="Chola Dynasty">Cholas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandyas" title="Pandyas">Pandyas</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">Southern India</a>. Like the Indonesian people above, the western sailors used the monsoon to cross the ocean. The unknown author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i> describes this route, as well as the commodities that were traded along various commercial ports on the coasts of the Horn of Africa and India circa 1 CE. Among these trading settlements were <a href="/wiki/Mosylon" title="Mosylon">Mosylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opone" title="Opone">Opone</a> on the Red Sea littoral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Periplus_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Periplus-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Discovery">Age of Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Age of Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2868" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/300px-Silk_route.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/450px-Silk_route.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/600px-Silk_route.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The economically important <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> was blocked from Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1453 with the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. This spurred exploration, and a new sea route around Africa was found, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1672" data-file-width="1540" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg/180px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg/270px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg/360px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_westward_routes.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1672" data-file-width="1540" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg/180px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg/270px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg/360px-Color_etopo1_ice_low_indian_ocean_eastward_routes.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Preferred sailing routes across the Indian Ocean</div></div></div></div>
<p>Unlike the Pacific Ocean where the civilization of the <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a> reached most of the far-flung islands and atolls and populated them, almost all the islands, archipelagos and atolls of the Indian Ocean were uninhabited until colonial times. Although there were numerous ancient civilizations in the coastal states of Asia and parts of Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> were the only island group in the Central Indian Ocean region where an ancient civilization flourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhivehi_people" title="Dhivehi people">Maldivians</a>, on their annual trade trip, took their oceangoing trade ships to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> rather than mainland India, which is much closer, because their ships were dependent of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Monsoon_Current" title="Indian Monsoon Current">Indian Monsoon Current</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arabic missionaries and merchants began to <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam#East_Africa" title="Spread of Islam">spread Islam along the western shores of the Indian Ocean</a> from the 8th century, if not earlier. A <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili</a> stone mosque dating to the 8th–15th centuries has been found in <a href="/wiki/Shanga,_Pate_Island" title="Shanga, Pate Island">Shanga</a>, Kenya. Trade across the Indian Ocean gradually introduced Arabic script and rice as a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple</a> in Eastern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
Muslim merchants traded an estimated 1000 African slaves annually between 800 and 1700, a number that grew to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 4000</span> during the 18th century, and 3700 during the period 1800–1870. Slave trade also occurred in the eastern Indian Ocean before the Dutch settled there around 1600 but the volume of this trade is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen-2017-overview_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-2017-overview-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1405 to 1433 admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> said to have led large fleets of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> on several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">treasure voyages</a> through the Indian Ocean, ultimately reaching the coastal countries of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/270px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/405px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png/540px-Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png 2x" width="270"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Descobrimentos_e_explora%C3%A7%C3%B5es_portuguesesV2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>For most of the 16th century, the Portuguese dominated the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Portuguese navigator <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> rounded the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> during his first voyage in 1497 and became the first European to sail to India. The <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a> he encountered along the African east coast lived in a series of cities and had established trade routes to India and to China. Among them, the Portuguese kidnapped most of their pilots in coastal raids and onboard ships. A few of the pilots, however, were gifts by local Swahili rulers, including the sailor from Gujarat, a gift by a <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> ruler in Kenya, who helped the Portuguese to reach India. In expeditions after 1500, the Portuguese attacked and colonised cities along the African coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
European slave trade in the Indian Ocean began when Portugal established <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_India" title="Portuguese India">Estado da Índia</a> in the early 16th century. From then until the 1830s, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 200</span> slaves were exported from Mozambique annually and similar figures has been estimated for slaves brought from Asia to the Philippines during the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Union" title="Iberian Union">Iberian Union</a> (1580–1640).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen-2017-overview_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-2017-overview-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> began its expansion into the Indian Ocean in 1517 with the conquest of Egypt under Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a>. Although the Ottomans shared the same religion as the trading communities in the Indian Ocean the region was unexplored by them. Maps that included the Indian Ocean had been produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_geographers" title="Muslim geographers">Muslim geographers</a> centuries before the Ottoman conquests; Muslim scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> in the 14th Century, had visited most parts of the known world; contemporarily with Vasco da Gama, Arab navigator <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_M%C4%81jid" title="Ahmad ibn Mājid">Ahmad ibn Mājid</a> had compiled a guide to navigation in the Indian Ocean; the Ottomans, nevertheless, began their own parallel era of discovery which rivalled the European expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> in the early 17th century lead to a quick increase in the volume of the slave trade in the region; there were perhaps up to 500,000 slaves in various <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch colonies</a> during the 17th and 18th centuries in the Indian Ocean. For example, some 4000 African slaves were used to build the <a href="/wiki/Fort_(Colombo)" title="Fort (Colombo)">Colombo fortress</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Ceylon" title="Dutch Ceylon">Dutch Ceylon</a>. Bali and neighbouring islands supplied regional networks with <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 100,000–150,000</span> slaves 1620–1830. Indian and Chinese slave traders supplied Dutch Indonesia with perhaps 250,000 slaves during the 17th and 18th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen-2017-overview_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-2017-overview-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> (EIC) was established during the same period and in 1622 one of its ships carried slaves from the <a href="/wiki/Coromandel_Coast" title="Coromandel Coast">Coromandel Coast</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a>. The EIC mostly traded in African slaves but also some Asian slaves purchased from Indian, Indonesian and Chinese slave traders. The French established colonies on the islands of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="Réunion">Réunion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> in 1721; by 1735 some 7,200 slaves populated the <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Islands" title="Mascarene Islands">Mascarene Islands</a>, a number which had reached 133,000 in 1807. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a> captured the islands in 1810, however, and because the British had <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_1807" title="Slave Trade Act 1807">prohibited the slave trade</a> in 1807 a system of clandestine slave trade developed to bring slaves to French planters on the islands; in all 336,000–388,000 slaves were exported to the Mascarene Islands from 1670 until 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen-2017-overview_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-2017-overview-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In all, European traders exported 567,900–733,200 slaves within the Indian Ocean between 1500 and 1850 and almost that same amount were exported from the Indian Ocean to the Americas during the same period. Slave trade in the Indian Ocean was, nevertheless, very limited compared to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 12,000,000</span> slaves exported across the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen-2017-overview_83-4"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-2017-overview-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mal%C3%A9_(8685996006).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg/220px-Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg/330px-Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg/440px-Mal%C3%A9_%288685996006%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mal%C3%A9_(8685996006).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mal%C3%A9" title="Malé">Malé</a>'s population has increased from 20,000 people in 1987 to more than 220,000 people in 2020.</div></div></div>
<p>Scientifically, the Indian Ocean remained poorly explored before the <a href="/wiki/International_Indian_Ocean_Expedition" title="International Indian Ocean Expedition">International Indian Ocean Expedition</a> in the early 1960s. However, the <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition"><i>Challenger</i> expedition</a> 1872–1876 only reported from south of the polar front. The <a href="/wiki/Valdivia_Expedition" title="Valdivia Expedition"><i>Valdivia</i> expedition</a> 1898–1899 made deep samples in the Indian Ocean. In the 1930s, the John Murray Expedition mainly studied shallow-water habitats. The <a href="/wiki/Albatross_expedition" title="Albatross expedition">Swedish Deep Sea Expedition</a> 1947–1948 also sampled the Indian Ocean on its global tour and the Danish <a href="/wiki/Galathea_expeditions" title="Galathea expeditions"><i>Galathea</i></a> sampled deep-water fauna from Sri Lanka to South Africa on its second expedition 1950–1952. The Soviet research vessel <a href="/wiki/RV_Vityaz_(1939)" title="RV Vityaz (1939)"><i>Vityaz</i></a> also did research in the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demo-etal-intro_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Demo-etal-intro-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> opened in 1869 when the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> dramatically changed global shipping – the sailing ship declined in importance as did the importance of European trade in favour of trade in East Asia and Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
The construction of the canal introduced many non-indigenous species into the Mediterranean. For example, the goldband goatfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Upeneus_moluccensis" title="Upeneus moluccensis">Upeneus moluccensis</a></i>) has replaced the red mullet (<i><a href="/wiki/Mullus_barbatus" title="Mullus barbatus">Mullus barbatus</a></i>); since the 1980s huge swarms of <a href="/wiki/Scyphozoa" title="Scyphozoa">scyphozoan</a> jellyfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Rhopilema_nomadica" title="Rhopilema nomadica">Rhopilema nomadica</a></i>) have affected tourism and fisheries along the Levantian coast and clogged power and desalination plants. Plans announced in 2014 to <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal_Area_Development_Project" title="Suez Canal Area Development Project">build a new, much larger Suez Canal</a> parallel to the 19th-century canal will most likely boost the economy in the region but also cause ecological damage in a much wider area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diego_garcian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1172" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Diego_garcian.jpg/220px-Diego_garcian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Diego_garcian.jpg/330px-Diego_garcian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Diego_garcian.jpg/440px-Diego_garcian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diego_garcian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unnamed <a href="/wiki/Chagossians" title="Chagossians">Chagossian</a> on <a href="/wiki/Diego_Garcia" title="Diego Garcia">Diego Garcia</a> in 1971 shortly before the British <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Chagossians" title="Expulsion of the Chagossians">expelled</a> the islanders when the island became a U.S. military base. The man spoke a <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French-based creole language</a> and his ancestors were most likely brought to the uninhabited island as slaves in the 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the colonial era, islands such as <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> were important shipping nodes for the Dutch, French, and British. Mauritius, an inhabited island, became populated by slaves from Africa and <a href="/wiki/Indian_indenture_system" title="Indian indenture system">indenture labour</a> from India. The end of <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_in_World_War_II" title="Indian Ocean in World War II">World War II</a> marked the end of the colonial era. The British left Mauritius in 1974 and with 70% of the population of Indian descent, Mauritius became a close ally of India. In the 1980s, during the Cold War, the South African regime acted to destabilise several island nations in the Indian Ocean, including the Seychelles, Comoros, and Madagascar. India intervened in Mauritius to prevent a coup d'état, backed up by the United States who feared the Soviet Union could gain access to <a href="/wiki/Port_Louis" title="Port Louis">Port Louis</a> and threaten the U.S. base on <a href="/wiki/Diego_Garcia" title="Diego Garcia">Diego Garcia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Iranrud" title="Iranrud">Iranrud</a> is an unrealised plan by Iran and the Soviet Union to build a canal between the Caspian Sea and the Persian Gulf.
</p><p>Testimonies from the colonial era are stories of African slaves, Indian indentured labourers, and white settlers. But, while there was a clear racial line between free men and slaves in the Atlantic World, this delineation is less distinct in the Indian Ocean — there were Indian slaves and settlers as well as black indentured labourers. There were also a string of prison camps across the Indian Ocean, such as <a href="/wiki/Cellular_Jail" title="Cellular Jail">Cellular Jail</a> in the Andamans, in which prisoners, exiles, POWs, forced labourers, merchants, and people of different faiths were forcefully united. On the islands of the Indian Ocean, therefore, a trend of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creolisation" title="Creolisation">creolisation</a> emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 26 December 2004 fourteen countries around the Indian Ocean were hit by a wave of <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a> caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">2004 Indian Ocean earthquake</a>. The waves radiated across the ocean at speeds exceeding 500 km/h (310 mph), reached up to 20 m (66 ft) in height, and resulted in an estimated 236,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 2000s, the ocean evolved into a hub of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia" title="Piracy in Somalia">pirate</a> activity. By 2013, attacks off the Horn region's coast had steadily declined due to active private security and international navy patrols, especially by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Navy" title="Indian Navy">Indian Navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malaysian_Airlines_Flight_370" title="Malaysian Airlines Flight 370">Malaysian Airlines Flight 370</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a> airliner with 239 persons on board, disappeared on 8 March 2014 and is alleged to have crashed into the southern Indian Ocean about 2,500 km (1,600 mi) from the coast of southwest <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>. Despite an extensive search, the whereabouts of the remains of the aircraft is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sentinelese" title="Sentinelese">Sentinelese</a> people of <a href="/wiki/North_Sentinel_Island" title="North Sentinel Island">North Sentinel Island</a>, which lies near <a href="/wiki/South_Andaman_Island" title="South Andaman Island">South Andaman Island</a> in the Bay of Bengal, have been called by experts the most <a href="/wiki/Uncontacted_peoples" title="Uncontacted peoples">isolated people</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sovereignty of the <a href="/wiki/Chagos_Archipelago" title="Chagos Archipelago">Chagos Archipelago</a> in the Indian Ocean is <a href="/wiki/Chagos_Archipelago_sovereignty_dispute" title="Chagos Archipelago sovereignty dispute">disputed</a> between the United Kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In February 2019, the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> issued an advisory opinion <a href="/wiki/Legal_Consequences_of_the_Separation_of_the_Chagos_Archipelago_from_Mauritius_in_1965" title="Legal Consequences of the Separation of the Chagos Archipelago from Mauritius in 1965">stating</a> that the UK must transfer the Chagos Archipelago to Mauritius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indian_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shipping_routes.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Shipping_routes.png/220px-Shipping_routes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Shipping_routes.png/330px-Shipping_routes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Shipping_routes.png/440px-Shipping_routes.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shipping_routes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major <a href="/wiki/Maritime_transport" title="Maritime transport">ocean trade routes</a> in the world includes the northern Indian Ocean.</div></div></div>
<p>The sea lanes in the Indian Ocean are considered among the most strategically important in the world with more than 80 percent of the world's seaborne trade in oil transits through the Indian Ocean and its vital chokepoints, with 40 percent passing through the Strait of Hormuz, 35 percent through the Strait of Malacca and 8 percent through the Bab el-Mandab Strait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Indian Ocean provides major sea routes connecting the Middle East, Africa, and East Asia with Europe and the Americas. It carries a particularly heavy traffic of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and petroleum products from the oil fields of the Persian Gulf and Indonesia. Large reserves of hydrocarbons are being tapped in the offshore areas of Saudi Arabia, Iran, India, and Western Australia. An estimated 40% of the world's offshore oil production comes from the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-2018_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-2018-3">[3]</a></sup> Beach sands rich in heavy <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, and offshore placer deposits are actively exploited by bordering countries, particularly India, Pakistan, South Africa, Indonesia, Sri Lanka, and Thailand.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3749" data-file-width="5623" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg/220px-A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg/330px-A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg/440px-A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_general_view_of_Mombasa_Port_on_Kenya%27s_Indian_Ocean_coast.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> Port on Kenya's Indian Ocean coast</div></div></div>
<p>In particular, the maritime part of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> leads through the Indian Ocean on which a large part of the global container trade is carried out. The Silk Road runs with its connections from the Chinese coast and its large container ports to the south via <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> via the Sri Lankan <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> opposite the southern tip of India via <a href="/wiki/Mal%C3%A9" title="Malé">Malé</a>, the capital of the Maldives, to the East African <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, from there to <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, then through the Red Sea over the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> into the Mediterranean, there via <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> to the Upper Adriatic to the northern Italian junction of <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> with its international free port and its rail connections to <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Silk Road has become internationally important again on the one hand through European integration, the end of the Cold War and free world trade and on the other hand through Chinese initiatives. Chinese companies have made investments in several Indian Ocean ports, including <a href="/wiki/Gwadar" title="Gwadar">Gwadar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hambantota" title="Hambantota">Hambantota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonadia" title="Sonadia">Sonadia</a>. This has sparked a debate about the strategic implications of these investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> There are also Chinese investments and related efforts to intensify trade in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and in European ports such as <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
